        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285"><meta property="og:title" content="Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c03285.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Layered sodium manganese-based oxides are appealing cathode candidates due to their high capacity and cost-effectiveness, yet performance degradation related with unwanted structural evolution still remains a disturbing disadvantage. Herein, atomic resolution STEM (scanning transmission electron microscopy) images of Na-extracted Na2/3NixCo1/3–xMn2/3O2 (x = 0, 1/6, 1/3) are collected and analyzed, to decipher the effect of cobalt and nickel substitution on the structural integrity of layered manganese-based oxides. Cobalt substitution is demonstrated to alleviate the lattice stress and retain the layered structure after sodium removal, and only a local P2-to-O2 phase transition could be identified. By contrast, various types of defects and phase transformation, including rarely reported P2-to-O3, are discovered in the Ni-substituted oxides. The generation of spinel and rock-salt phases is the critical evidence of cation mixing that leads to unrecoverable capacity loss. The interplay of different transition metals is complex, and compositional optimization is encouraged to minimize the effect of the concomitant phase transition."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c03285.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187e93ad1d075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e93ad1d075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'c188fc74-1295-43ae-8586-12944a4958bb'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e93ad1d075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

	</li>
</ul>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187e93ad1d075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Clarifying the Roles of Cobalt and Nickel in the Structural Evolution of Layered Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/medium/nl1c03285_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Layered sodium manganese-based oxides are appealing cathode candidates due to their high capacity and cost-effectiveness, yet performance degradation related with unwanted structural evolution still remains a disturbing disadvantage. Herein, atomic resolution STEM (scanning transmission electron microscopy) images of Na-extracted Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>1/3–<i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 1/6, 1/3) are collected and analyzed, to decipher the effect of cobalt and nickel substitution on the structural integrity of layered manganese-based oxides. Cobalt substitution is demonstrated to alleviate the lattice stress and retain the layered structure after sodium removal, and only a local P2-to-O2 phase transition could be identified. By contrast, various types of defects and phase transformation, including rarely reported P2-to-O3, are discovered in the Ni-substituted oxides. The generation of spinel and rock-salt phases is the critical evidence of cation mixing that leads to unrecoverable capacity loss. The interplay of different transition metals is complex, and compositional optimization is encouraged to minimize the effect of the concomitant phase transition.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D9619%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDuojie%2BWu%252C%2BXuming%2BYang%252C%2BShihui%2BFeng%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D21%26issueNum%3D22%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.1c03285%26title%3DClarifying%2Bthe%2BRoles%2Bof%2BCobalt%2Band%2BNickel%2Bin%2Bthe%2BStructural%2BEvolution%2Bof%2BLayered%2BCathodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D9624%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=P2-type+Layered+Oxides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_P2-type%20layered%20oxides">P2-type layered oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_structural%20evolution">structural evolution</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phase+Transformation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_phase%20transformation">phase transformation</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Green energy conversion and storage from renewable sources, such as wind and solar power, is embracing rapid development, which is related with the worldwide endeavors to reduce CO<sub>2</sub> emission and a joint pursuit of sustainable electricity generation. A huger and huger demand for electric energy storage systems is forecasted, and batteries are highly suitable for this application owing to their high energy density, high efficiency, and easy maintenance. Lithium ion batteries (LIB) are ubiquitously adopted in portable electronics, electric vehicles, and stationary energy storage. The immense applications of LIBs lead to a sharp price spike, and the concern about the supply of lithium resources is mounting. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Sodium ion batteries (SIB) share similar mechanisms with LIBs and can be built with abundant elements only. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> As an alternative technology of LIBs, SIBs hold great promise for stationary energy storage applications, where cost-effectiveness is a more critical consideration than gravimetrical capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 3 to 7">(3−7)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Growing interests are focused on electrode materials for SIBs that dictate the energy density and cycle stability. Layered sodium transition metal oxides (NaTMO) are attractive due to their high specific capacity and similar properties to their lithium counterparts. There are mainly two types of structures for NaTMO: P2-type (space group: <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>) and O3-type (space group: <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 8 to 12">(8−12)</a> “P” and “O” stand for the prismatic and octahedral occupation site of sodium and “2” and “3” the number of oxygen layers in a repeated stacking unit. Both of them could suffer from a series of phase transitions relevant with the stacking sequence changes or even collapse of layered structures. Sodium manganese-based oxides, which receive wide attention for the abundance of manganese resources, are more prone to a detrimental phase transition due to the Jahn–Teller effects of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> There are two typical strategies to stabilize the layered structure: one is to substitute partial manganese with other transition metals (Ti, Co, Fe, Ni, Zn, etc.); the other is to incorporate the charge carriers (Li, Mg, etc.) on the sodium sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> And the two strategies can be combined. For example, the cathode Na<sub>0.7</sub>Mg<sub>0.05</sub>[Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Mg<sub>0.15</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> features smooth voltage profiles and high structural integrity during cycling, which is attributed to the magnesium occupation in both the sodium and transition metal sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The research activity on sodium cobalt- and nickel-substituted manganese-based oxides is intensive. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref18" aria-label="reference 13 and 18">(13,18)</a> On one hand, both cobalt and nickel are redox active, and their valence state change can offer some extra capacity. On the other hand, the analogous binary and ternary oxides in lithium ion batteries are well developed and commercialized. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 19 and 20">(19,20)</a> There are already some typical cases where P2-type layered oxides could deliver advanced cycle performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> Nevertheless, their detailed structural evolution, especially unambiguous identification of defects and phase transition at the atomic level, still remains to be elucidated, which would be of high significance for an in-depth understanding of their structural characteristics and the conceptualization of rational designing.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we prepared three kinds of P2-type layered sodium manganese-based oxides, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>1/3–<i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 1/6, 1/3), to investigate the structural evolution upon the removal of sodium ions. By virtue of scanning transmission electron microscopic (STEM) characterization in high-angle annual dark-field (HAADF) and annual bright-field (ABF) modes, the atomic resolution structures of the layered oxides after sodium removal are imaged, and various types of structural evolutions (crack, kink, hole, dislocation, phase transition, etc.) are observed and analyzed.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Three kinds of sodium manganese-based oxides, with a general formula of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>1/3–<i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 1/6, 1/3), are prepared from a solid-state reaction between transition metal carbonates and sodium carbonate, and their X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. They are a close match to the XRD pattern of P2-type layered Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (PDF #54-0894), which confirms that all the synthesized samples adopt a pure P2-type structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The <i>d</i>-spacing of the (002) planes is thus calculated to be 0.56 nm. To investigate the robustness of the layered structure after removal of sodium ions at high voltage, the samples are assembled into coin cells and charged to 4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, which enables electrochemical sodium ion extraction. According to the charging profiles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), the number of extracted sodium ions is evaluated to be about 0.56.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in the HAADF-STEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>) of Na-extracted Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NaCoMnO), the layered structure is perfectly preserved, and the energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) elemental mapping result (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) indicates a uniform Na–Co–Mn–O distribution. In other words, Co substitution is a feasible strategy to counter the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. By contrast, nanocracks are clearly identified in the HAADF-STEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>) of Na-extracted Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NaNiMnO). In addition, there are regions obviously brighter than their surrounding areas (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c</a>), which indicates severe segregation of transition metals (Ni and Mn are much brighter than Na and O in STEM images). It can be recognized from the EDX mapping result that the signal of Ni in the bright region is relatively stronger, while that of Na is lower. According to the vertical line profile of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3c and S5</a>), the counts of Ni and Mn in the bright region are far above the average value indeed, and that of Na is below average. It is supposed to be caused by local overcharging and cation mixing in NaNiMnO.</div><div class="NLM_p">In another high-resolution HAADF-STEM image of Na-extracted NaNiMnO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>), five typical types of structural evolution (including dislocation, kink, phase transition, hole, and cracks) are identified and framed in different colors in the small area about 50 nm × 50 nm. Dislocation is referred to the lattice mismatch, or rather, discontinuity of TMO layers, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b, which could be induced by lattice strain created by ununiform interlayer contraction. It could be regarded as relative movement perpendicular to the stacking TMO slab. The movement parallel to the stacking layers, namely, layer gliding, could alter the alignment of oxygen and transition metals (TMs), which leads to a phase transition and is commonly observed in the vicinity of the crack (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). Detailed phase transition mechanisms will be discussed later. These two kinds of movement could incubate substantial structural evolutions and collectively lead to the generation of intragranular kinks, holes, and cracks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d–f). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref24" aria-label="reference 20 and 24">(20,24)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/medium/nl1c03285_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. HAADF-STEM image of (a) Na-extracted NaNiMnO and (b–f) magnified areas framed in different colors representing different types of structural evolutions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The difference in the structural evolution is naturally related with the different extents of volume contraction upon sodium extraction. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> shows the high-resolution HAADF-STEM images of NaCoMnO, NaNiMnO, and a Co/Ni cosubstituted ternary oxide, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Co<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NaNiCoMnO), charged to 4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. In the case of Na-extracted NaCoMnO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b), uniformly stacked layers are observed, and an average spacing is measured to be 0.56 nm, which is basically identical to the (002) <i>d</i>-spacing of pristine NaCoMnO. As for the Na-extracted NaNiMnO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d), there are distorted regions up to 7 layers (about 3.5 nm in width) and regular regions with an average spacing about 0.51 nm, suggesting that NaNiMnO undergoes severer structural shrinkage. The distorted regions shown in the HAADF-STEM image of NaNiCoMnO are relatively smaller than that of NaNiMnO, which is clearly reflected from the narrower valley in the vertical line profile (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e,f) and is supposed to be the embryonic form of cracks. It is also discovered that the interlayer distance of locally regular regions fluctuates in a small range from 0.51 to 0.56 nm. It can be concluded that partial substitution of Ni by Co in NaNiMnO is favorable to alleviate lattice strain and sustain the structural integrity, and this could be rationalized by the Jahn–Teller effect of generated Ni<sup>4+</sup> upon sodium extraction. The interlayer distance change after sodium extraction should be attributed to phase transitions.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/medium/nl1c03285_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. HAADF-STEM images and corresponding vertical line profiles of (a, b) NaCoMnO, (c, d) NaNiMnO, and (e, f) NaNiCoMnO charged to 4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Co substitution turns out to be an effective way to suppress the distortion of the layered structure. In the HAADF-STEM image of Na-extracted NaCoMnO showing a very large area (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/suppl_file/nl1c03285_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), uniform lattice fringes are observed, and no dislocation could be recognized. After careful checking of all the collected images, we do find areas showing the pristine P2 phase and the transited O2 phase from layer gliding, and their HAADF-STEM images are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,c, respectively. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, the TM ions are arranged in an ααα-stacking model corresponding to the P2 phase. The elemental contrast is related to the atomic number with a <i>Z</i><sup>1/3</sup> dependency in the annular bright-field (ABF) imaging mode, which is more sensitive to light elements than in the HAADF imaging mode. Herein, the ABF-STEM image of the P2 phase is provided as <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b, where sodium and oxygen atoms are directly observed, and the prismatic occupation of sodium is confirmed. In the HAADF-STEM image of the O2 phase, the TM ions adopt the <i>αβαβ</i>-stacking model, and the octahedral oxygen coordination is identified in the ABF-STEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). It should also be noticed that the P2-to-O2 phase transition, which is caused by a slight layer gliding, does not bring about a notable change of interlayer distance.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/medium/nl1c03285_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. HAADF-STEM images and corresponding ABF-STEM images of (a, b) the P2 phase and (c, d) the O2 phase in the Na-extracted NaCoMnO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It is commonly believed that the phase transition from P2 to O2 is energetically more preferential than that from P2 to O3; the former could be realized by a slight layer gliding while the latter requires a rotation of TMO layers. The P2-to-O3 phase transition is rarely reported in the literature, but its possibility still cannot be denied. The two phases could be easily distinguished based on the arrangement of TM ions recognized as bright spots in STEM images. By contrast with NaCoMnO, both NaNiMnO and NaNiCoMnO suffer from various kinds of phase transitions. In the high-resolution HAADF-STEM image of Na-extracted NaNiCoMnO (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>), both P2 and O3 phases are identified. The TM ions in the red-framed region are packed in an <i>ααα</i>-stacking model corresponding to the P2-type structure, unlike that in the blue-framed region <i>αβγα-</i>stacking model. The interlayer distance of the O3 phase is only 0.51 nm, much smaller than that of the P2 phase. Owing to the difference in the interlayer distances, the local P2-to-O3 phase transition could generate shear stress, eventually lead to the formation of dislocation, and even crack.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/medium/nl1c03285_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Atomic resolution HAADF-STEM image of Na-extracted NaNiCoMnO. P2- and O3-type structure models are displaced in the left and right sides, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Except for the phase transition between different layered structures (P2, O2, O3), a spinel phase is identified in the atomic-level HAADF-STEM image of Na-extracted NaNiCoMnO and the enlarged view of the red-framed area (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). The spinel phase has a tetragonal structure with a space group of <i>I</i>4<sub>1</sub>/<i>mmd</i> (<i>a</i> = 6.229 Å), and its appearance is the evidence of a cation mixing process and the migration of TM ions into the sodium layers in the Ni-substituted layered oxide. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c shows dislocations in atomic resolution, near which distortion and phase transition can be recognized. In the case of Co-free NaNiMnO, the structural evolution is similar. Distinct distortion and phase transition are found near the dislocation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d,e). In the enlarged view of a local area (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d), rock-salt and spinel phases are identified, both of which support the viewpoint of cation mixing phenomena. It could result in dissolution of TM ions or formation of inactive phases, which partially explains the degradation mechanism of such layered cathode materials. In the near-crack regions with a width of about 3 nm, the TM arrangement does not match the structure models of all the above-mentioned arrangements, which means that phase transition does occur, and more underlying transition mechanisms remain to be elucidated. These phases (O3, spinel, rock-salt, etc.) produced after sodium extraction are only observed in the Ni-substituted samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f), and it might be correlated with the generation of Jahn–Teller-active Ni<sup>4+</sup> after sodium extraction.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/medium/nl1c03285_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) HAADF-STEM images of Na-extracted NaNiCoMnO and enlarged views of (b) red- and (c) yellow-framed regions; HAADF-STEM images of charged NaNiMnO showing (d) edge dislocation, (e) near-crack phase transition region, and (f) different identified phases.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285/asset/images/large/nl1c03285_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c03285&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">To conclude, STEM characterization is utilized to investigate the structural evolution of P2-type Mn-based oxides, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>1/3–<i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 1/6, 1/3), charged to 4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. It is found that the layered structure of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Co<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is perfectly maintained after sodium extraction, despite that the P2-to-O2 phase transition, which is usually reversible, occurred locally. By contrast, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> undergoes various types of phase transitions, and they are correlated with the formation of defects, including dislocation, cracks, kinks, holes, and so on. Together with the partial retention of the P2 phase of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> after sodium extraction, phase transitions to O2, O3, spinel, and rock-salt phases are identified. It should be emphasized that the P2-to-O3 phase transition, which required a layer rotation rather than gliding, is an important discovery. Through the high-resolution STEM images of dislocation and nanocracks at the atomic level, it is witnessed that the dislocation or nanocrack is usually associated with phase transitions. For the ternary cathode, Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Co<sub>1/6</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, complex phase transitions are also demonstrated, but the observed cracks are much smaller than that of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. It is reasonable to infer that Co substitution is favorable to the stabilization of P2-type layered structure, while Ni substitution is unable to prevent structural contraction, irreversible phase transitions, dislocation, or crack generation after sodium extraction, and the situation is even worse when the Ni content is high. We hope the atomic presentation of various defects and phase transitions in this work could facilitate researchers in the battery community to further understand the complex structural evolution of layered oxides and devise innovative strategies accordingly.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2010.11.074"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J. B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Challenges for rechargeable batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges for rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, J. B.; Kim, Youngsik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">196</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6688-6694</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Strategies for Li-ion batteries that are based on lithium-insertion compds. as cathodes are limited by the capacities of the cathode materials and by the safe charging rates for Li transport across a passivating solid electrolyte interface layer on a carbon-based anode.  With these strategies, it is difficult to meet the com. constraints on Li-ion batteries for plug-in-hybrid and all-elec. vehicles as well as those for stationary elec. energy storage (EES) in a grid.  Existing alternative strategies include a gaseous O2 electrode in a Li/air battery and a solid sulfur (S8) cathode in a Li/S battery.  We compare the projected energy densities and EES efficiencies of these cells with those of a third alternative, a Li/Fe(III)/Fe(II) cell contg. a redox couple in an aq. soln. as the cathode.  Preliminary measurements indicate proof of concept, but implementation of this strategy requires identification of a suitable Li+-ion electrolyte.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C4CP02957G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural evolution of the cathode during cycling plays a vital role in the electrochem. performance of sodium-ion batteries.  A strategy based on engineering the crystal structure coupled with chem. substitution led to the design of the layered P2@P3 integrated spinel oxide cathode Na0.5Ni0.1Co0.15Mn0.65Mg0.1O2, which shows excellent sodium-ion half/full battery performance.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIB) are being intensively investigated, owing to the natural abundance and low cost of Na resources.  However, the SIBs still suffer from poor rate capability due to the large ionic radius of Na+ ion and the significant kinetic barrier to Na+-ion transport.  Here, we present an Fd-3m spinel-integrated P2-type layered composite (P2 + Fd-3m) material as a high-rate cathode for SIBs.  The P2 + Fd-3m composite material Na0.50Ni1/6Co1/6Mn2/3O2 shows significantly enhanced discharge capacity, energy d., and rate capability as compared to the pure P2-type counterpart.  The composite delivers a high capacity of 85 mA h g-1 when discharging at a very high c.d. of 1500 mA g-1 (10 C rate) between 2.0 and 4.5 V, validating it as a promising cathode candidate for high-power SIBs.  The superior performance is ascribed to the improved kinetics in the presence of the integrated-spinel phase, which facilitates fast electron transport to coordinate with the timely Na+-ion insertion/extn.  The findings of this work also shed light on the importance of developing lattice doping, surface coating, and electrolyte additives to further improve the structural and interfacial stability of P2-type cathode materials and fully realize their practical applications in sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/0378-4363(80)90214-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouassier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagenmuller, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structural classification and properties of the layered oxides</span>. <i>Physica B+C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1980</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>99</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1-4</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">81</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">85</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High performance manganese-based layered oxide cathodes: overcoming the challenges of sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ortiz-Vitoriano, Nagore; Drewett, Nicholas E.; Gonzalo, Elena; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Currently, there is increasing interest in developing 'beyond lithium' battery technologies to augment, or in certain situations replace, lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  Room temp. sodium ion batteries (NIBs) offer an attractive combination of low cost and plentiful constituents and a wide range of phases, structures and stoichiometries available for optimization.  Sodium layered oxides are considered to be promising candidates as cathode materials, due to their flexibility and versatility, as well as their intrinsically fast structural diffusion of Na ions which leads to enhanced rate capability.  In particular, sodium manganese based layered oxides (generally NaxMn1-y-zMyTMzO2, where TM represents one or more transition metals, and M consists of one or more non-transition metals) are a key family of materials, in part due to the relatively low cost and environmentally friendly nature of the manganese, and consequently are worthy of a detailed investigation.  Examn. of these systems, particularly in terms of stoichiometry and phase, has shown that significant advances have been made recently, both in terms of understanding the mechanisms behind electrochem. performance, and in terms of refining these to produce improved materials.  The goal of this review is to present the current developments in sodium manganese based layered oxides (particularly with respect to electrochem. performance, phys. properties and manganese content), to discuss the current state of this field of research and to draw conclusions regarding where future investigations may be most fruitfully directed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA12441H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ni- and/or Mn-based layered transition metal oxides as cathode materials for sodium ion batteries: status, challenges and countermeasures</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ni- and/or Mn-based layered transition metal oxides as cathode materials for sodium ion batteries: status, challenges and countermeasures</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Shenghan; Sun, Chenglin; Wang, Ning; Zhang, Qichun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been attracting great interest as alternatives for grid and mobile energy storage applications in the modern world due to the sufficient natural abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  To accelerate the practical application of SIB systems, the electrochem. performance of cathode materials should be further improved to satisfy the increasing worldwide demand.  As promising cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition metal oxides (LTMOs) exhibit high specific capacity and high energy d. due to their appropriate voltage window.  Here, recent progress and achievements of three representative LTMOs including Ni-based, Mn-based, and Ni/Mn co-based cathodes in terms of the relationship between structural design and electrochem. performance are summarized, aiming at cost redn. and performance improvement.  The insights in this review focus on the development of LTMOs as cathodes for SIBs including overall battery performance, prepn. strategies and operation mechanism, therefore speeding up the commercialization of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C7EE00827A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, G.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, C.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Heald, S.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights into the structural effects of layered cathode materials for high voltage sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Gui-Liang; Amine, Rachid; Xu, Yue-Feng; Liu, Jianzhao; Gim, Jihyeon; Ma, Tianyuan; Ren, Yang; Sun, Cheng-Jun; Liu, Yuzi; Zhang, Xiaoyi; Heald, Steve M.; Solhy, Abderrahim; Saadoune, Ismael; Mattis, Wenjuan Liu; Sun, Shi-Gang; Chen, Zonghai; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cathode materials are crit. to the energy d., power d. and safety of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Herein, we performed a comprehensive study to elucidate and exemplify the interplay mechanism between phase structures, interfacial microstrain and electrochem. properties of layered-structured NaxNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode materials for high voltage SIBs.  The electrochem. test results showed that NaxNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 with an intergrowth P2/O3/O1 structure demonstrates better electrochem. performance and better thermal stability than NaxNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 with P2/O3 binary-phase integration and NaxNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 where only the P phase is dominant.  This result is caused by the distinct interfacial microstrain development during the synthesis and cycling of the P2/O3/O1 phase.  In operando high energy X-ray diffraction further revealed that the intergrowth P2/O1/O3 cathode can inhibit the irreversible P2-O2 phase transformation and simultaneously improve the structure stability of the O3 and O1 phases during cycling.  We believe that interfacial microstrain can serve as an indispensable bridge to guide future design and synthesis of high performance SIB cathode materials and other high energy battery materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701785"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manthiram, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Progress in High-Voltage Cathode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tuning P2-Structured Cathode Material by Na-Site Mg Substitution for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Qin-Chao; Meng, Jing-Ke; Yue, Xin-Yang; Qiu, Qi-Qi; Song, Yun; Wu, Xiao-Jing; Fu, Zheng-Wen; Xia, Yong-Yao; Shadike, Zulipiya; Wu, Jinpeng; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Zhou, Yong-Ning</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">141</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">840-848</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides suffer from multiple voltage plateaus, due to Na+/vacancy-order superstructures caused by strong interplay between Na-Na electrostatic interactions and charge ordering in the transition metal layers.  Here, Mg ions are successfully introduced into Na sites in addn. to the conventional transition metal sites in P2-type Na0.7[Mn0.6Ni0.4]O2 as new cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Mg ions in the Na layer serve as "pillars" to stabilize the layered structure, esp. for high-voltage charging, meanwhile Mg ions in the transition metal layer can destroy charge ordering.  More importantly, Mg ion occupation in both sodium and transition metal layers will be able to create "Na-O-Mg" and "Mg-O-Mg" configurations in layered structures, resulting in ionic O 2p character, which allocates these O 2p states on top of those interacting with transition metals in the O-valence band, thus promoting reversible oxygen redox.  This innovative design contributes smooth voltage profiles and high structural stability.  Na0.7Mg0.05[Mn0.6Ni0.2Mg0.15]O2 exhibits superior electrochem. performance, esp. good capacity retention at high current rate under a high cutoff voltage (4.2 V).  A new P2 phase is formed after charge, rather than an O2 phase for the unsubstituted material.  Besides, multiple intermediate phases are obsd. during high-rate charging.  Na-ion transport kinetics are mainly affected by elemental-related redox couples and structural reorganization.  These findings will open new opportunities for designing and optimizing layer-structured cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/nano.202000030"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Manganese-based layered oxide cathodes for sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>Nano Select</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">2</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">200</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">225</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
